<div id="review_1551820473" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1551820473" />
    <a title="Joslyn Allen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/29956038-joslyn-allen"><img alt="Joslyn Allen" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1551820473?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joslyn Allen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joslyn Allen" href="/user/show/29956038-joslyn-allen">Joslyn Allen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29956038-joslyn-allen?shelf=women-s-voices">women-s-voices</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29956038-joslyn-allen?shelf=poc">poc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29956038-joslyn-allen?shelf=immigration">immigration</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1551820473" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4832822567520326285">Edwidge Danticat writes with sophistication beyond her years and wmediumith an ethereal beauty. It is unbelievable to me that someone can produce works of such maturity and grace as "Breath, Eyes, Memory" and "the farming of bones" before the age of 30. <br/><br/> In "the farming of bones" Danticat takes her readers to the other side of her native island of Hispaniola, laying bare the oppression and desperation of Haitian immigrants in the Dominican Republic of the 1930s. Here she explores the trials of a</span>
  <span id="freeText4832822567520326285" style="display:none">Edwidge Danticat writes with sophistication beyond her years and wmediumith an ethereal beauty. It is unbelievable to me that someone can produce works of such maturity and grace as "Breath, Eyes, Memory" and "the farming of bones" before the age of 30. <br /><br /> In "the farming of bones" Danticat takes her readers to the other side of her native island of Hispaniola, laying bare the oppression and desperation of Haitian immigrants in the Dominican Republic of the 1930s. Here she explores the trials of a servant class, the violence implicit to the work of migrant cane harvesters, and the pervasive trauma of refugees. Peopled by characters whose voices are all but snuffed out, this novel eloquently captures the human need for a voice.<br /><br />"Taking turns, they exchanged tales quickly, the haste in their voices sometimes blurring the words, for greater than their desire to be heard was the hunger to tell. One could hear it in the fervor of the declarations, the obscenities shouted when something could<br />not be remembered fast enough, when a stutter allowed another speaker to race into his own account without the stutterer having completed his."<br /><br />These voiceless immigrants, too, face prejudice and exclusion that is frighteningly familiar to today's political rhetoric. <br /><br />"'I pushed my son out of my body here, in this country,' one woman said in a mix of Alegrian Kreyol and Spanish, the tangled language of those who aways stuttered as they spoke, caught as they were on the narrow ridge between two nearly native tongues. 'My mother too pushed me out of her body here. Not me, not my son, not one of us has ever seen the other side of the border. Still they won't put our birth papers in our palms so my son can have knowledge placed into his head by a proper educator in a proper school."<br /><br />But "the farming of bones" isn't just a story of political turmoil and class struggle. It is a love story full of a passion that brings heat to the cheeks of the reader, not out of modesty or embarrassment, but out of empathy because of how exquisitely Danticat writes. The protagonist Amabelle feels an intense, once in a lifetime chemistry with her lover; "For some, passion is the gift of a ring in a church ceremony, the bearing of children as shared property. For me it was just a smile I couldn't help, tugging at the sides of my face." Her connection to Sebastian cuts to her core: <br /><br />"I'm afraid to be shy, distant, and cold. I am afraid I cease to exist when he's not there. I'm like one of those sea stones that sucks its colors inside and loses its translucence once it's taken out into the sun, out of the froth of the waves. When he's not there, I'm afraid I know no one and no one knows me."<br /><br />This work is a braid of timeless romance and (unfortunately) timeless struggle. It is, like her earlier work, a gift to breath, eyes, and memory. Danticat is a force to be reckoned with and hopefully one who continues to write for many years to come.<br /><br />More at chronicbibliophilia.wordpress.com</span>
  <a data-text-id="4832822567520326285" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_723400053" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/723400053" />
    <a title="Lucinda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3431786-lucinda"><img alt="Lucinda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304291505p2/3431786.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/723400053?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lucinda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lucinda" href="/user/show/3431786-lucinda">Lucinda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 1569471266" href="/book/show/376829.The_Farming_of_Bones">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3431786-lucinda?shelf=caribbean">caribbean</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3431786-lucinda?shelf=contemporary-fiction">contemporary-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer723400053" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3418285905462578080">This is a book about suffering, surviving. Living through events so much bigger than us that they swallow us whole. Coming out the other side, there is nothing left that is recognizable and no symbol, marker, or sign powerful enough to represent what has been lost. Where are the traces of loss, can something or someone who was really such a big part of our lives just disappear so entirely? No two ways about it, this is a raw harrowing tale of survival.<br/><br/>I didn't know much about the relations betwe</span>
  <span id="freeText3418285905462578080" style="display:none">This is a book about suffering, surviving. Living through events so much bigger than us that they swallow us whole. Coming out the other side, there is nothing left that is recognizable and no symbol, marker, or sign powerful enough to represent what has been lost. Where are the traces of loss, can something or someone who was really such a big part of our lives just disappear so entirely? No two ways about it, this is a raw harrowing tale of survival.<br /><br />I didn't know much about the relations between Haiti and the Dominican republic, other than that it was less than friendly. Two very different colonial histories sharing one small island. This novel tells something of the fallout of these divergent colonial paths in the form of the Parsley Massacre, where Haitians living and working in the Dominican were slaughtered by the Dominican army and swarms of suddenly hostile former neighbors. It is said that Haitians' inability to correctly pronounce the word 'parsley' in Spanish was a means to separate them out for death by machete. A cruelty that is bone-chilling to say the least.<br /><br />Danticat tells the story of this event through the eyes of a servant girl Amabelle, who is living a somewhat fortunate life in a small village near the border with Haiti. She is aware of the injustices and suffering that her compatriots who work in the cane fields suffer, but pushes this to the back of her mind, because her personal relations with her employers are more or less decent. All of this is turned upside down when a personal tragedy is quickly followed by a wave of public violence, and Amabelle finds herself struggling to find a way to survive. <br /><br />While I found the writing a bit clumsy in parts Danticat is certainly effective in evoking the disorientation and utter incomprehension that one must experience in the face of this kind of mass violence. And her attempts to show how difficult it is for a person to come to grips with what comes after this type of trauma is hauntingly moving.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3418285905462578080" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review723400053">
  <td colspan="2">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1264002611" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1264002611" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37256808-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444088588p2/37256808.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1264002611?book_show_action=true">Apr 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/37256808-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37256808-tim?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1264002611" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4189991593142551620">Danticat has received a lot of attention, and is possibly the first female Haitian-American writer of note. This book is set in the infamous “ethnic cleansing” (to use a current term) that occurred along the border of Haiti and the Dominican Republic in 1937 or 1938. The DR dictater Trujillo ordered the slaughter of a large number of Haitians (the exact number is unclear, but it may have been around 12,000 – 15,000) in response to growing tensions between the two peoples in the DR’s border areas</span>
  <span id="freeText4189991593142551620" style="display:none">Danticat has received a lot of attention, and is possibly the first female Haitian-American writer of note. This book is set in the infamous “ethnic cleansing” (to use a current term) that occurred along the border of Haiti and the Dominican Republic in 1937 or 1938. The DR dictater Trujillo ordered the slaughter of a large number of Haitians (the exact number is unclear, but it may have been around 12,000 – 15,000) in response to growing tensions between the two peoples in the DR’s border areas. The story centers on a young woman named Amabelle who works as a maid in the home of a Dominican military officer. The trouble is foreshadowed when a Haitian is run over by a car the officer is driving to see his newborn son and daughter. When the trouble begins, Amabelle runs away and has a series of adventures along the border as she and her friends try to avoid being killed by the Dominicans. <br /><br />It works as an adventure and as a depiction of a historical event, but the book has some shortcomings. For one thing, Amabelle, although she is very capable and brave and independent in the fashion of feminist heroines, has very little personality. Her voice is muffled, and she seems unstimulated by what goes on around her. One feels no closer to her by the end of the book than at the beginning. And the ending feels a bit forced and adds little to the morality or sense of the story. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4189991593142551620" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="June" class="user" itemprop="url" name="June" href="/user/show/20234777-june">June</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 1569471266" href="/book/show/376829.The_Farming_of_Bones">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20234777-june?shelf=library-finds">library-finds</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20234777-june?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20234777-june?shelf=caribbean-reads">caribbean-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2282146092" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10194801062666808627">I am late to the game-Recently there seemed to be a lot of hype for Edwidge Danticat. Then she received 2017 Neustadt International Prize for Literature, comparable to the Nobel Prize. So I decided to rectify my ignorance on her writing. <br/><br/>I was blown away. She paints images with words in this harrowing novel about Haitian genocide in 1937 in Dominican Republic. To me it was quintessential Caribbean writing. She was able to evoke country life and such detailed characters with few words. It is such</span>
  <span id="freeText10194801062666808627" style="display:none">I am late to the game-Recently there seemed to be a lot of hype for Edwidge Danticat. Then she received 2017 Neustadt International Prize for Literature, comparable to the Nobel Prize. So I decided to rectify my ignorance on her writing. <br /><br />I was blown away. She paints images with words in this harrowing novel about Haitian genocide in 1937 in Dominican Republic. To me it was quintessential Caribbean writing. She was able to evoke country life and such detailed characters with few words. It is such a lyrical prose that compels reading inspite of the plot. It was such a familiar story even though I have little knowledge of these two Caribbean countries. The ending felt disjointed from the story and for that reason it failed to get the 5⭐️ which I felt it almost deserved. An excellent narrative on loss, immigrant experience, colorism and poverty. <br /><br />Highly recommend this one. There is not a scrap of any Haitian stereotypes in this novel. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10194801062666808627" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17284498432542798496">"He opens his mouth a few more times and moans. <br/>'If you let yourself,' he says finally, 'you can see it before your eyes, a boy carrying his dead father from the road, wobbling, swaying, stumbling under the weight.   <span id="freeText17284498432542798496" style="display:none">"He opens his mouth a few more times and moans. <br />'If you let yourself,' he says finally, 'you can see it before your eyes, a boy carrying his dead father from the road, wobbling, swaying, stumbling under the weight. The boy with the wind in his ears and pieces of the tin roofs that opened the father's throat blowing around him. The boy trying not to drop the father, not crying or screaming like you'd think, but praying that more of the fathers blood will stay in the father's throat and not go into the muddy floor, going no one knows where. If you let yourself, you can see it before your eyes."<br /><br />"..All monuments of this great size are built with human blood.."</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('zuOFXf1iba3pSWFBo2YWfO11z4nf5o+xEgnEIxD+RWiho1TLEO8P/FUrR9Dv+p1yw2gtyCwhsDfNxnHVsMkxnw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9Bc2D5FP00wPz7WXeOUTH5twjSSKXFADQOPFPkgvn+CbV+eZfMKxHbOtkwY0eZgRtW1vZXmbb4WfLHDI6BjrFw==')})
  }
//]]>

A skillful blend of fact and fiction, In the Time of the Butterflies is inspired by the true story of the three Mirabal sisters who, in 1960, were murdered for their part in an underground plot to overthrow the government. Alvarez breathes life into these historical figures--known as &quot;las mariposas,&quot; or &quot;the butterflies,&quot; in the underground--as she imagines their teenage years, their gradual involvement with the revolution, and their terror as their dissentience is uncovered.  Alvarez\'s controlled writing perfectly captures the mounting tension as &quot;the butterflies&quot; near their horrific end. The novel begins with the recollections of Dede, the fourth and surviving sister, who fears abandoning her routines and her husband to join the movement. Alvarez also offers the perspectives of the other sisters: brave and outspoken Minerva, the family\'s political ringleader; pious Patria, who forsakes her faith to join her sisters after witnessing the atrocities of the tyranny; and the baby sister, sensitive Maria Teresa, who, in a series of diaries, chronicles her allegiance to Minerva and the physical and spiritual anguish of prison life. But as Carlos and Virginia grow up under the same roof, they become deeply and covertly attached to one another.When Peter discovers the pair’s secret and accuses Carlos of a heinous crime, it is up to a brusque, insensitive English inspector to discover the truth.           <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3525.Edwidge_Danticat"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1226496700p3/3525.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/3525.Edwidge_Danticat">Edwidge Danticat
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,957
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer462165239088006468">Edwidge Danticat was born in Haiti and moved to the United States when she was twelve. She is the author of several books, including <em>Breath, Eyes, Memory</em>, an Oprah Book Club selection; <em>Krik? Krak!</em>, a National Book Award finalist; and <em>The Farming of Bones</em>, an American Book Award winner. She is also the editor of <em>The Butterfly's Way: Voices from the Haitian Dyaspora in the United States</em> and <em>The Beac</em></span>
  <span id="freeText462165239088006468" style="display:none">Edwidge Danticat was born in Haiti and moved to the United States when she was twelve. She is the author of several books, including <em>Breath, Eyes, Memory</em>, an Oprah Book Club selection; <em>Krik? Krak!</em>, a National Book Award finalist; and <em>The Farming of Bones</em>, an American Book Award winner. She is also the editor of <em>The Butterfly's Way: Voices from the Haitian Dyaspora in the United States</em> and <em>The Beacon Best of 2000: Great Writing by Men and Women of All Colors and Cultures</em>.<br /><br />Danticat earned a degree in French Literature from Barnard College, where she won the 1995 Woman of Achievement Award, and later an MFA from Brown University. She lives in Miami with her husband and daughters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="462165239088006468" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
